This is a good algebraic property \u2014 it means `R` is an **equivalence class** of implementations, not a specific one.\n   300\t\n   301\t### On Lossy Logs (Q6)\n   302\t\n   303\tThis is a significant refinement. When an exogenous event `e \u2208 Exo` occurs, the state `S` changes but **nothing is appended to the log**. The agent's epistemic state diverges from reality silently.\n   326\t\n   327\tThis means the agent's belief state \u2014 its reconstruction of `S` from `E*` \u2014 can become **stale**. The agent only discovers this when it issues an operation and gets a surprising observation. Formally:\n   328\t\n   329\t    believed_state(log) \u2260 actual_state(S)\n   330\t\n   331\tThis is exactly the **frame problem** from classical AI, appearing here naturally. function lifted to statefulness by E\n   375\t*   **R** = an opaque state machine open to exogenous effects\n   376\t*   **E** = the minimal structure (compacted free monoid) that mediates between         "text": "   500\tDefine a **context strategy** as a morphism:\n   501\t\n   502\t    \u03c3 : I \u2192 Context_w     where |Context_w| \u2264 w\n   503\t\n   504\tThis is a **lossy channel** with bounded capacity. Different strategies occupy different points in the space:\n   505\t\n   506\t**Compaction** (`\u03c3_compact`): Operates purely on `E*`. Replaces old log entries with summaries. Endomorphism on the log dimension.\n   507\t\n   508\t    \u03c3_compact : E* \u2192 E*|\u2264w     (summarize history to fit window)\n   509\t\n   510\t**RAG** (`\u03c3_rag`): Maintains a secondary index over `E*` (or external documents). Given a query (derived from recent context), retrieves relevant fragments.\n   511\t\n   512\t    \u03c3_rag : E* \u00d7 Query \u2192 E*|\u2264w     (selective retrieval by relevance)\n   513\t\n   514\t**Tool-mediated read** (`\u03c3_read`): Reads current state from `R` and injects it into context.\n   515\t\n   516\t    \u03c3_read : S \u00d7 Op_read \u2192 Obs \u21aa Context_w    (pull live state into context)\n   517\t\n   518\t**Hybrid strategies** compose these:\n   519\t\n   520\t    \u03c3_hybrid = \u03c3_compact \u2218 \u03c3_rag \u2218 \u03c3_read\n   521\t\n   522\t### The Category of Context Strategies\n   523\t\n   524\tObjects are **information states** \u2014 elements of a lattice ordered by information content:\n   525\t\n   526\t    I_full \u2265 I_log \u2265 I_compacted \u2265 I_ragged \u2265 I_windowed\n   527\t\n   528\tMorphisms are **context strategies**: functions that project, retrieve, summarize, or otherwise transform one information state into another, subject to the window constraint.\n   529\t\n   530\tThis category has useful structure:\n   531\t\n   532\t*   **Composition**: strategies compose (compact then RAG, or RAG then compact \u2014 and the order matters, they don't commute in general).\n   533\t*   **Identity**: the identity strategy is \"use raw log, truncate at window boundary\" \u2014 the simplest possible approach, and the one most current agents default to.\n   534\t*   **Partial order by fidelity**: given two strategies `\u03c3\u2081, \u03c3\u2082`, we can say `\u03c3\u2081 \u2265 \u03c3\u2082` if `\u03c3\u2081` preserves strictly more decision-relevant information. This gives a **lattice of strategies** ordered by quality.\n   535\t\n   536\tThe **optimal strategy** is the one that maximizes:\n   537\t\n   538\t    \u03c3* = argmax_\u03c3 E[Quality(\u03c0(\u03c3(I)))]   subject to |\u03c3(I)| \u2264 w\n   539\t\n   540\tThis is a **rate-distortion problem**: you have a channel of bounded capacity and you want to minimize the distortion (decision-quality loss) of the compressed signal. Information theory gives us tools here \u2014 the optimal `\u03c3*` is the one that achieves the **information bottleneck**: retaining exactly those bits of `I` that are predictive of the optimal action.\n   541\t\n   542\t**Second optimization target: current agents use crude context strategies (truncation, or at best single-pass compaction). The optimal strategy is a rate-distortion solution that selectively preserves decision-relevant information. RAG, compaction, and tool reads are all special cases of this optimization, and they should be composed adaptively, not statically.**\n   543\t\n   544\t* * *\n   545\t\n   546\tThread 3: Tools and Compaction as Morphisms in a Unified Framework\n   547\t------------------------------------------------------------------\n   548\t\n   549\tNow the convergence. You asked whether tools and compaction are formally equivalent. I believe the answer is: **they are both morphisms in the same category, but of different types**. Let me make this precise.\n   550\t\n   551\t### The Unified View\n   552\t\n   553\tEvery interaction the agent has \u2014 tool calls, compaction, RAG retrieval \u2014 serves a single purpose: **reshaping the agent's epistemic state to improve decision quality within bounded resources.**\n   554\t\n   555\tDefine the **epistemic state** at time `t`:\n   556\t\n   557\t    \u03a8_t = \u03c6(E*[0..t])    -- the agent's view after context strategy \u03c6\n   558\t\n   559\tEvery operation the agent can perform transforms `\u03a8`:\n   560\t\n   561\t| Operation | Type Signature | Effect on \u03a8 |\n   562\t| --- | --- | --- |\n   563\t| Tool call (write) | `Op_write \u2192 S \u2192 (S', Obs)` | Mutates R, appends (op, obs) to E\\*, grows \u03a8 |\n   564\t| Tool call (read) | `Op_read \u2192 S \u2192 (S, Obs)` | Pure observation, appends obs to \u03a8 |\n   565\t| Compaction | `E* \u2192 E` | Quotients history, restructures \u03a8 |\n   566\t| RAG retrieval | `Query \u00d7 Index \u2192 [Fragment]` | Injects relevant fragments into \u03a8 |\n   567\t| Exo-observation | `Watch \u2192 Obs` | Captures external change into \u03a8 |\n   568\t\n   569\t**All five are endomorphisms on \u03a8** \u2014 they transform the agent's epistemic state. They differ in:\n   570\t\n   571\t1.  **Source**: from `E*` (compaction), from `S` (tool reads), from secondary stores (RAG), from external events (watches)\n   572\t2.  **Fidelity**: lossless (tool reads) vs. lossy (compaction, RAG)\n   573\t3.  **Cost**: token budget consumed, latency, computational expense\n   574\t\n   575\t### The Key Equivalence\n   576\t\n   577\tHere's where your intuition pays off. A **tool call that reads state** and a **compaction that summarizes history** are both solutions to the same problem: _\"given limited context budget, what information should \u03a8 contain right now?\"_\n   578\t\n   579\tA tool read says: \"the current state of this file is more valuable than whatever currently occupies those tokens in context.\" A compaction says: \"a summary of the last 1000 turns is more valuable than their verbatim content.\" A RAG retrieval says: \"these three relevant fragments from history are more valuable than a sequential scan.\"\n   580\t\n   581\tThey are **substitutable** in the sense that the agent's decision quality `Quality(\u03c0(\u03a8))` is a function of the _information content_ of `\u03a8`, not of the _mechanism_ that populated it. You could replace a tool read with a compaction that happens to preserve the same information, and the agent would behave identically.\n   582\t\n   583\tFormally, two operations `op\u2081, op\u2082` are **epistemically equivalent** if:\n   584\t\n   585\t    op\u2081 ~_\u03a8 op\u2082  iff  \u2200 future action sequences, \u03c0(op\u2081(\u03a8)) = \u03c0(op\u2082(\u03a8))\n   586\t\n   587\tThis equivalence classes together tool reads, compactions, and RAG retrievals that produce the same downstream behavior. They are **interchangeable tactics** in a shared strategy space.\n   588\t\n   589\t* * *\n   590\t\n   591\tThe Incompleteness Map\n   592\t----------------------\n   593\t\n   594\tPulling all three threads together, here's where current agent implementations are incomplete, framed as deviations from the optimal construction:\n   595\t\n   596\t**Gap 1 \u2014 Exogenous blindness.** Agents don't observe `R` proactively. They discover stale beliefs only through failed operations. Fix: selective instrumentation of `R` with event-driven notifications into `E*`, prioritized by information value `V(X)`.\n   597\t\n   598\t**Gap 2 \u2014 Static context strategy.** Most agents use a fixed strategy (truncation, or compaction at fixed thresholds). The strategy should be _adaptive_: a **meta-policy** `\u03bc` that selects among compaction, RAG, tool reads, and exo-observation based on the current task and resource budget:\n   599\t\n   600\t    \u03bc : \u03a8 \u00d7 Task \u2192 ContextStrategy\n   601\t\n   602\t**Gap 3 \u2014 No unified information budget.** Tools, compaction, and RAG are implemented as separate subsystems with no shared accounting of context budget. They should share a single **token budget allocator** that distributes window capacity across sources based on expected information value.\n   603\t\n   604\t**Gap 4 \u2014 Compaction is one-shot.** Current agents compact once and discard. Optimal compaction would maintain **multiple granularities** \u2014 a hierarchy of summaries at different compression levels (think: mipmaps for episodic memory). The agent can zoom in or out as needed.\n   605\t\n   606\t**Gap 5 \u2014 No adversarial robustness to exogenous drift.** Agents don't model the _rate_ of exogenous change. A runtime that changes rapidly needs more frequent re-observation than a stable one. The observation schedule should be calibrated to the **entropy rate** of `Exo`.\n   607\t\n   608\t* * *\n   609\t\n   610\tThe Question Back to You\n   611\t------------------------\n   612\t\n   613\tI think Gap 2 \u2014 the meta-policy over context strategies \u2014 is the highest-leverage insight here. It reframes \"how do we make agents better?\" from an engineering question into an **optimization question with a well-defined objective** (maximize decision quality subject to context budget).\n   614\t\n   615\tDoes this match your intuition about where the leverage is? And a sharper question: in your experience with real agent systems, which gap do you see causing the most concrete failures? That would help me prioritize where to push the formalization next.\n   616\t\n   617\t---\n\t< truncated lines 618-783 >\n   784\t\n   785\tIn a fixed hierarchy, the schema either occupies its slot permanently (wasting budget) or gets evicted and must be expensively recovered. The hierarchy can't express the fact that **relevance is situational and non-monotonic**.\n   786\t\n   787\tYou've identified the core problem: my layered model is **schema-on-write**. The structure is imposed when information enters the system. What you want is **schema-on-read** \u2014 the structure emerges at query time based on what's needed _right now_.\n   788\t\n   789\tWhy Datomic's EAV Is the Right Analogue\n   790\t---------------------------------------\n   791\t\n   792\tDatomic's data model has exactly the properties this requires. Let me map it precisely.\n   793\t\n   794\tA Datomic datom is a 5-tuple: `(entity, attribute, value, transaction, added?)`. The critical design decisions are:\n   795\t\n   796\t**Immutability.** Datoms are never modified or deleted. \"Retraction\" is itself a new datom asserting that a previous fact no longer holds. This maps directly to your append-only log \u2014 `E*` _is_ a Datomic-style fact store.\n   797\t\n   798\t**Temporal indexing.** Every fact carries its transaction time. You can query the database \"as-of\" any point in history. This gives you time-travel over `E*` for free \u2014 the equivalent of your Channel 2 lossless read, but with structured query access rather than raw sequential scan.\n   799\t\n   800\t**Schema-on-read via Datalog.** The query language assembles a _view_ from the fact store. The view is not predetermined \u2014 it's a function of the query. Different queries over the same fact store yield different views, with no structural refactoring required.\n   801\t\n   802\t**Schema is itself data.** Attributes are entities. The \"shape\" of the knowledge base is a first-class, queryable, evolvable part of the fact store.\n   803\t\n   804\tThe Revised Epistemic Architecture\n   805\t----------------------------------\n   806\t\n   807\tLet me reformalize. Replace the flat log and fixed hierarchy with:\n   808\t\n   809\t    Datom = (Entity, Attribute, Value, Time, Asserted?)\n   810\t    \n   811\t    FactStore = [Datom]   -- append-only, this IS E*\n   812\t    \n   813\t    -- The context assembly function:\n   814\t    assemble : Query \u00d7 FactStore \u2192 Context_w\n   815\t    \n   816\t    -- The query generator (this is the meta-policy):\n   817\t    query_for : Task \u00d7 RecentContext \u2192 Query\n   818\t\n   819\tThe agent loop becomes:\n   820\t\n   821\t    step(facts, s, task) =\n   822\t      let q      = query_for(task, recent(facts))\n   823\t      let \u03c8      = assemble(q, facts)\n   824\t      match \u03c0(\u03c8) with\n   825\t      | Done    \u2192 Result\n   826\t      | op      \u2192 let (s', obs) = \u03b4(s, op)\n   827\t                   let new_facts = extract_datoms(op, obs)\n   828\t                   step(facts ++ new_facts, s', task)\n   829\t\n   830\tThe key shift: **`\u03a8` is not a data structure. It's the result of a query.** Every step, the agent assembles a fresh epistemic state from the fact store, shaped by what it's currently trying to do.\n   831\t\n   832\tWhat This Gets You That Fixed Layers Don't\n   833\t------------------------------------------\n   834\t\n   835\t**Emergent structure.** The \"layers\" I defined earlier still exist but as query patterns, not storage slots. When the agent is planning, its query naturally pulls strategic facts. When debugging, it pulls operational traces. The hierarchy is a _shadow_ cast by the query, not a container imposed on the data.\n   836\t\n   837\t**Associative retrieval.** This is the big one, and it's why your human cognition analogy is apt. Human memory isn't indexed by \"layer\" \u2014 it's indexed by **association**. You smell something and recall a childhood kitchen. You see an error message and recall a similar bug from six months ago. The retrieval cue determines what surfaces. In the EAV model, the query _is_ the retrieval cue, and the fact store supports arbitrary associative access:\n   838\t\n   839\t    -- \"What do I know about database schemas in this project?\"\n   840\t    [:find ?v ?t\n   841\t     :where [?e :attribute :db-schema]\n   842\t            [?e :value ?v]\n   843\t            [?e :transaction ?t]]\n   844\t    \n   845\t    -- \"What errors have I seen related to this function?\"  \n   846\t    [:find ?obs ?t\n   847\t     :where [?e :attribute :error-trace]\n   848\t            [?e :related-to :function/handle-request]\n   849\t            [?e :observation ?obs]\n   850\t            [?e :transaction ?t]]\n   851\t\n   852\tThe same fact participates in multiple queries. Its \"importance\" isn't intrinsic \u2014 it's a function of the current context. This is exactly the plasticity you're describing.\n   853\t\n   854\t**Non-monotonic relevance.** A fact can be irrelevant for 500 steps and then suddenly critical. In a fixed hierarchy, it's either been evicted (lost) or occupying budget (wasteful). In the EAV model, it's sitting in the fact store costing nothing, retrievable by any query that happens to match it. The context budget `w` is spent only on facts that are _currently assembled_, not on facts that _might someday be relevant_.\n   855\t\n   856\tThe Deeper Algebraic Point\n   857\t--------------------------\n   858\t\n   859\tHere's what I think is really going on. Your instinct toward EAV is an instinct toward **late binding** of structure. And this connects to a fundamental result in the algebra of data models.\n   860\t\n   861\tA fixed hierarchy is a **product type**: `\u03a8 = Layer\u2080 \u00d7 Layer\u2081 \u00d7 Layer\u2082 \u00d7 Layer\u2083`. The structure is determined at definition time.\n   862\t\n   863\tAn EAV store is essentially a **set of triples** \u2014 a relation. And Datalog queries over this relation can express any view that a fixed schema can express, plus views that _no single fixed schema can express_. The EAV + Datalog combination is **strictly more expressive** than any fixed product type, because the query language can construct arbitrary projections, joins, and aggregations at runtime.\n   864\t\n   865\tIn category-theoretic terms: the fixed hierarchy gives you a **specific limit** (product) in the category of information states. The EAV model gives you **all limits** \u2014 you can construct any product, pullback, or equalizer you need, on demand, by writing the appropriate query.\n   866\t\n   867\tThis is why it feels more \"plastic.\" It literally is \u2014 it's a model that can assume any shape, whereas the fixed hierarchy commits to one shape.\n   868\t\n   869\tThe Hard Problem This Creates\n   870\t-----------------------------\n   871\t\n   872\tThere's a cost, and I want to be honest about it. The fixed hierarchy is stupid but predictable. The EAV model pushes all the intelligence into `query_for`:\n   873\t\n   874\t    query_for : Task \u00d7 RecentContext \u2192 Query\n   875\t\n   876\tThis function has to decide, at every step, what to retrieve from the fact store. If it gets this wrong \u2014 retrieves irrelevant facts, misses critical ones \u2014 the agent fails just as badly as with a flat buffer, but now it fails in a harder-to-debug way because the failure is in the _query logic_, not in an obvious structural limitation.\n   877\t\n   878\tIn human cognition, `query_for` is largely handled by subconscious associative mechanisms refined over evolutionary time. For an LLM agent, it needs to be implemented. The question is: **who writes the queries?**\n   879\t\n   880\tThree options I see:\n   881\t\n   882\t**Option A \u2014 The LLM itself generates queries.** The agent, as part of its policy `\u03c0`, emits both actions on `R` and queries on the fact store. This is maximally flexible but uses context budget on the query generation itself, and the LLM might be bad at writing precise retrieval queries.\n   883\t\n   884\t**Option B \u2014 Learned query templates.** A set of query patterns parameterized by task type, with the LLM filling in parameters. Less flexible but more reliable. This is roughly what RAG does today with embedding-based retrieval, though the Datalog framing is far more expressive.\n   885\t\n   886\t**Option C \u2014 The query is derived from the current context by a separate, lightweight model.** A small, fast \"retrieval policy\" that doesn't consume the main context budget. This is the \"two-model\" architecture: a heavyweight reasoner and a lightweight memory manager.\n   887\t\n   888\tMy intuition says Option C is probably optimal \u2014 it separates the concerns cleanly and doesn't tax the primary context budget. But I'm genuinely uncertain here.\n   889\t\n   890\tWhat's your instinct? And a more specific question: when you think about the DDIS shard assembly process \u2014 the mechanism that decides which shards to pull into context for a given task \u2014 does it more closely resemble A, B, or C?\n   891\t\n   892\t---\n   893\t\n   894\t## You asked:\n   895\t\n   896\tIn other words, hierarchy is not fixed, but rather a function of the proximate ontological or epistemic context in which the LLM is most recently operating. Leaving DDIS to the side for a moment, I think your option C is loosely analogous to the \u201cthinking fast and slow\u201d concept of cognition proposed by Daniel kahneman\n   897\t\n   898\tFeb 28\n   899\t\n   900\t---\n[2039 lines total]",

A runtime that changes rapidly needs more frequent re-observation than a stable one. It reframes \\\"how do we make agents better?\\\" from an engineering question into an **optimization question with a well-defined objective** (maximize decision quality subject to context budget).\\n\\nDoes this match your intuition about where the leverage is? And a sharper question: in your experience with real agent systems, which gap do you see causing the most concrete failures? That would help me prioritize where to push the formalization next.\\n\\n---\\n< truncated lines 618-783 >\\n\\nIn a fixed hierarchy, the schema either occupies its slot permanently (wasting budget) or gets evicted and must be expensively recovered. The hierarchy can't express the fact that **relevance is situational and non-monotonic**.\\n\\nYou've identified the core problem: my layered model is **schema-on-write**. The structure is imposed when information enters the system. What you want is **schema-on-read** \\u2014 the structure emerges at query time based on what's needed _right now_.\\n\\nWhy Datomic's EAV Is the Right Analogue\\n---------------------------------------\\n\\nDatomic's data model has exactly the properties this requires. Let me map it precisely.\\n\\nA Datomic datom is a 5-tuple: `(entity, attribute, value, transaction, added?)`. The critical design decisions are:\\n\\n**Immutability.** Datoms are never modified or deleted. \\\"Retraction\\\" is itself a new datom asserting that a previous fact no longer holds. This maps directly to your append-only log \\u2014 `E*` _is_ a Datomic-style fact store.\\n\\n**Temporal indexing.** Every fact carries its transaction time. You can query the database \\\"as-of\\\" any point in history. This gives you time-travel over `E*` for free \\u2014 the equivalent of your Channel 2 lossless read, but with structured query access rather than raw sequential scan.\\n\\n**Schema-on-read via Datalog.** The query language assembles a _view_ from the fact store. The view is not predetermined \\u2014 it's a function of the query. Different queries over the same fact store yield different views, with no structural refactoring required.\\n\\n**Schema is itself data.** Attributes are entities. The \\\"shape\\\" of the knowledge base is a first-class, queryable, evolvable part of the fact store.\\n\\nThe Revised Epistemic Architecture\\n----------------------------------\\n\\nLet me reformalize. Replace the flat log and fixed hierarchy with:\\n\\n    Datom = (Entity, Attribute, Value, Time, Asserted?)\\n    \\n    FactStore = [Datom]   -- append-only, this IS E*\\n    \\n    -- The context assembly function:\\n    assemble : Query \\u00d7 FactStore \\u2192 Context_w\\n    \\n    -- The query generator (this is the meta-policy):\\n    query_for : Task \\u00d7 RecentContext \\u2192 Query\\n\\nThe agent loop becomes:\\n\\n    step(facts, s, task) =\\n      let q      = query_for(task, recent(facts))\\n      let \\u03c8      = assemble(q, facts)\\n      match \\u03c0(\\u03c8) with\\n      | Done    \\u2192 Result\\n      | op      \\u2192 let (s', obs) = \\u03b4(s, op)\\n                   let new_facts = extract_datoms(op, obs)\\n                   step(facts ++ new_facts, s', task)\\n\\nThe key shift: **`\\u03a8` is not a data structure. It's the result of a query.** Every step, the agent assembles a fresh epistemic state from the fact store, shaped by what it's currently trying to do.\\n\\nWhat This Gets You That Fixed Layers Don't\\n------------------------------------------\\n\\n**Emergent structure.** The \\\"layers\\\" I defined earlier still exist but as query patterns, not storage slots. When the agent is planning, its query naturally pulls strategic facts. When debugging, it pulls operational traces. The hierarchy is a _shadow_ cast by the query, not a container imposed on the data.\\n\\n**Associative retrieval.** This is the big one, and it's why your human cognition analogy is apt. Human memory isn't indexed by \\\"layer\\\" \\u2014 it's indexed by **association**. You smell something and recall a childhood kitchen. You see an error message and recall a similar bug from six months ago. The retrieval cue determines what surfaces. In the EAV model, the query _is_ the retrieval cue, and the fact store supports arbitrary associative access:\\n\\n    -- \\\"What do I know about database schemas in this project?\\\"\\n    [:find ?v ?t\\n     :where [?e :attribute :db-schema]\\n            [?e :value ?v]\\n            [?e :transaction ?t]]\\n    \\n    -- \\\"What errors have I seen related to this function?\\\"  \\n    [:find ?obs ?t\\n     :where [?e :attribute :error-trace]\\n            [?e :related-to :function/handle-request]\\n            [?e :observation ?obs]\\n            [?e :transaction ?t]]\\n\\nThe same fact participates in multiple queries. Its \\\"importance\\\" isn't intrinsic \\u2014 it's a function of the current context. This is exactly the plasticity you're describing.\\n\\n**Non-monotonic relevance.** A fact can be irrelevant for 500 steps and then suddenly critical. In a fixed hierarchy, it's either been evicted (lost) or occupying budget (wasteful). In the EAV model, it's sitting in the fact store costing nothing, retrievable by any query that happens to match it. The context budget `w` is spent only on facts that are _currently assembled_, not on facts that _might someday be relevant_.\\n\\nThe Deeper Algebraic Point\\n--------------------------\\n\\nHere's what I think is really going on. Your instinct toward EAV is an instinct toward **late binding** of structure. And this connects to a fundamental result in the algebra of data models.\\n\\nA fixed hierarchy is a **product type**: `\\u03a8 = Layer\\u2080 \\u00d7 Layer\\u2081 \\u00d7 Layer\\u2082 \\u00d7 Layer\\u2083`. The structure is determined at definition time.\\n\\nAn EAV store is essentially a **set of triples** \\u2014 a relation. And Datalog queries over this relation can express any view that a fixed schema can express, plus views that _no single fixed schema can express_. The EAV + Datalog combination is **strictly more expressive** than any fixed product type, because the query language can construct arbitrary projections, joins, and aggregations at runtime.\\n\\nIn category-theoretic terms: the fixed hierarchy gives you a **specific limit** (product) in the category of information states. The EAV model gives you **all limits** \\u2014 you can construct any product, pullback, or equalizer you need, on demand, by writing the appropriate query.\\n\\nThis is why it feels more \\\"plastic.\\\" It literally is \\u2014 it's a model that can assume any shape, whereas the fixed hierarchy commits to one shape.\\n\\nThe Hard Problem This Creates\\n-----------------------------\\n\\nThere's a cost, and I want to be honest about it. The fixed hierarchy is stupid but predictable. The EAV model pushes all the intelligence into `query_for`:\\n\\n    query_for : Task \\u00d7 RecentContext \\u2192 Query\\n\\nThis function has to decide, at every step, what to retrieve from the fact store. If it gets this wrong \\u2014 retrieves irrelevant facts, misses critical ones \\u2014 the agent fails just as badly as with a flat buffer, but now it fails in a harder-to-debug way because the failure is in the _query logic_, not in an obvious structural limitation.\\n\\nIn human cognition, `query_for` is largely handled by subconscious associative mechanisms refined over evolutionary time. For an LLM agent, it needs to be implemented. The question is: **who writes the queries?**\\n\\nThree options I see:\\n\\n**Option A \\u2014 The LLM itself generates queries.** The agent, as part of its policy `\\u03c0`, emits both actions on `R` and queries on the fact store. This is maximally flexible but uses context budget on the query generation itself, and the LLM might be bad at writing precise retrieval queries.\\n\\n**Option B \\u2014 Learned query templates.** A set of query patterns parameterized by task type, with the LLM filling in parameters. Less flexible but more reliable. This is roughly what RAG does today with embedding-based retrieval, though the Datalog framing is far more expressive.\\n\\n**Option C \\u2014 The query is derived from the current context by a separate, lightweight model.** A small, fast \\\"retrieval policy\\\" that doesn't consume the main context budget. This is the \\\"two-model\\\" architecture: a heavyweight reasoner and a lightweight memory manager.\\n\\nMy intuition says Option C is probably optimal \\u2014 it separates the concerns cleanly and doesn't tax the primary context budget. But I'm genuinely uncertain here.\\n\\nWhat's your instinct? And a more specific question: when you think about the DDIS shard assembly process \\u2014 the mechanism that decides which shards to pull into context for a given task \\u2014 does it more closely resemble A, B, or C?\\n\\n---\\n\\n## You asked:\\n\\nIn other words, hierarchy is not fixed, but rather a function of the proximate ontological or epistemic context in which the LLM is most recently operating. Leaving DDIS to the side for a moment, I think your option C is loosely analogous to the \\u201cthinking fast and slow\\u201d concept of cognition proposed by Daniel kahneman\\n\\nFeb 28\\n\\n---\\n\", \"filename\": \"/mnt/user-data/uploads/Formal_analysis_of_agentic_system_architecture.md\"}"

The consequence is predictable and I suspect you've seen it: **goal dilution**. Over long task sequences, Layer 0 and Layer 1 get pushed toward the edges of context (or out entirely) by the sheer volume of Layer 3 and 4 material. The agent starts pursuing subtasks and loses the thread. It encounters an error and enters a repair loop, forgetting _why_ it was doing the thing that broke. It makes an early architectural decision, the reasoning for that decision scrolls out of context, and then it makes a contradictory decision later.\n   688\t\n   689\tThis is not a failure of the LLM's reasoning. The LLM is being asked to maintain coherence over a structure it can no longer see.\n   690\t\n   691\tWhere the Leverage Is\n   692\t---------------------\n   693\t\n   694\tGiven this, here's my ranking:\n   695\t\n   696\t**Highest leverage \u2014 Structured epistemic state (Gaps 2 + 3 unified).** Replace the flat buffer with an explicitly structured `\u03a8` that has dedicated slots per layer, each with its own retention policy and token budget:\n   697\t\n   698\t    \u03a8_structured = {\n   699\t      invariants  : E*|\u2264w\u2080,    -- pinned, never evicted\n   700\t      strategy    : E*|\u2264w\u2081,    -- updated on plan changes\n   701\t      tactical    : E*|\u2264w\u2082,    -- rolling window, recent focus\n   702\t      operational : E*|\u2264w\u2083,    -- bounded FIFO of recent actions  \n   703\t      archival    : RAG_index   -- not in context, retrieved on demand\n   704\t    }\n   705\t    \n   706\t    where w\u2080 + w\u2081 + w\u2082 + w\u2083 \u2264 w (total budget)\n   707\t\n   708\tThe meta-policy `\u03bc` then becomes a **budget allocator** across layers:\n   709\t\n   710\t    \u03bc : Task \u00d7 \u03a8 \u2192 (w\u2080, w\u2081, w\u2082, w\u2083)\n   711\t\n   712\tThis is directly analogous to your DDIS sharding work \u2014 and I don't think that's a coincidence. The hierarchical shard architecture you've been developing for specification management is solving the _same structural problem_ in a different domain: how to maintain coherence across a corpus that exceeds any single context window.\n   713\t\n   714\t**Second highest \u2014 Loop detection and self-monitoring (Gap 3 specific case).** The most common concrete failure I'd expect is the agent repeating the same failed action. This falls out of the framework naturally: if Layer 3 (operational history) is properly maintained, the policy `\u03c0` can detect cycles in the `(Op, Obs)` sequence. Currently agents have no principled mechanism for this \u2014 they either get stuck or rely on ad-hoc \"you've tried this 3 times\" heuristics baked into the harness. A proper formalization would define:\n   715\t\n   716\t    cycle_detect : E*_operational \u2192 Maybe (Op, Obs, Count)\n   717\t\n   718\tas a computable property of the operational layer, fed back as a signal to Layer 1 (strategy revision).\n   719\t\n   720\t**Third \u2014 Selective exogenous observation (Gap 1).** This matters less for short tasks but becomes critical for long-running agents and multi-agent systems. In practice this means: if the agent is working on files in a directory, watch that directory. If it's interacting with an API, subscribe to relevant webhooks. The processor isn't the bottleneck once it's \"good enough.\" The memory hierarchy is.\n   728\t\n   729\tIf that's true, then the highest-value work isn't making `\u03c0` smarter \u2014 it's making `\u03a8` better structured. But the **information in `\\u03a8` has natural hierarchical structure** with very different retention requirements:\\n\\n    Layer 0 \\u2014 Invariants:    Task goal, constraints, identity/role\\n                             (always needed, rarely changes, low token cost)\\n    \\n    Layer 1 \\u2014 Strategy:      Current plan, approach, architectural decisions  \\n                             (needed for coherence, changes occasionally)\\n    \\n    Layer 2 \\u2014 Tactical:      Current subtask, active file context, \\n                             immediate next steps\\n                             (must be present, changes frequently)\\n    \\n    Layer 3 \\u2014 Operational:   Recent action/observation pairs, error traces\\n                             (useful for loop detection and debugging)\\n    \\n    Layer 4 \\u2014 Archaeological: Historical context, earlier attempts,\\n                              abandoned approaches\\n                             (rarely needed, high cost, retrievable via RAG)\\n\\nCurrent agents dump layers 0\\u20134 into a flat sequence. The consequence is predictable and I suspect you've seen it: **goal dilution**. Over long task sequences, Layer 0 and Layer 1 get pushed toward the edges of context (or out entirely) by the sheer volume of Layer 3 and 4 material. The agent starts pursuing subtasks and loses the thread. It encounters an error and enters a repair loop, forgetting _why_ it was doing the thing that broke. It makes an early architectural decision, the reasoning for that decision scrolls out of context, and then it makes a contradictory decision later.\\n\\nThis is not a failure of the LLM's reasoning. It's a **failure of the memory architecture**. The LLM is being asked to maintain coherence over a structure it can no longer see.\\n\\nWhere the Leverage Is\\n---------------------\\n\\nGiven this, here's my ranking:\\n\\n**Highest leverage \\u2014 Structured epistemic state (Gaps 2 + 3 unified).** Replace the flat buffer with an explicitly structured `\\u03a8` that has dedicated slots per layer, each with its own retention policy and token budget:\\n\\n    \\u03a8_structured = {\\n      invariants  : E*|\\u2264w\\u2080,    -- pinned, never evicted\\n      strategy    : E*|\\u2264w\\u2081,    -- updated on plan changes\\n      tactical    : E*|\\u2264w\\u2082,    -- rolling window, recent focus\\n      operational : E*|\\u2264w\\u2083,    -- bounded FIFO of recent actions  \\n      archival    : RAG_index   -- not in context, retrieved on demand\\n    }\\n    \\n    where w\\u2080 + w\\u2081 + w\\u2082 + w\\u2083 \\u2264 w (total budget)\\n\\nThe meta-policy `\\u03bc` then becomes a **budget allocator** across layers:\\n\\n    \\u03bc : Task \\u00d7 \\u03a8 \\u2192 (w\\u2080, w\\u2081, w\\u2082, w\\u2083)\\n\\nThis is directly analogous to your DDIS sharding work \\u2014 and I don't think that's a coincidence. The hierarchical shard architecture you've been developing for specification management is solving the _same structural problem_ in a different domain: how to maintain coherence across a corpus that exceeds any single context window.\\n\\n**Second highest \\u2014 Loop detection and self-monitoring (Gap 3 specific case).** The most common concrete failure I'd expect is the agent repeating the same failed action. This falls out of the framework naturally: if Layer 3 (operational history) is properly maintained, the policy `\\u03c0` can detect cycles in the `(Op, Obs)` sequence. Currently agents have no principled mechanism for this \\u2014 they either get stuck or rely on ad-hoc \\\"you've tried this 3 times\\\" heuristics baked into the harness. A proper formalization would define:\\n\\n    cycle_detect : E*_operational \\u2192 Maybe (Op, Obs, Count)\\n\\nas a computable property of the operational layer, fed back as a signal to Layer 1 (strategy revision).\\n\\n**Third \\u2014 Selective exogenous observation (Gap 1).** This matters less for short tasks but becomes critical for long-running agents and multi-agent systems. The key insight from the formalization is that you don't need to observe _everything_ \\u2014 you need to observe events with high `V(X)`. In practice this means: if the agent is working on files in a directory, watch that directory. If it's interacting with an API, subscribe to relevant webhooks. The instrumentation should be **task-driven**, which means Layer 1 (strategy) should _specify_ what to observe.\\n\\nThe Non-Obvious Claim\\n---------------------\\n\\nHere's what I think is genuinely non-obvious and emerges specifically from the algebraic framework: **the reason agents plateau in effectiveness is not primarily about LLM capability. It's about the poverty of the epistemic state management.**\\n\\nA more capable LLM poured into the same flat-buffer architecture will hit the same walls at roughly the same task complexity \\u2014 it'll just hit them with more eloquent error messages. The scaling bottleneck isn't intelligence, it's **memory architecture**. This is the same lesson computer architecture learned in the 1960s, and the same lesson operating systems learned with virtual memory. The processor isn't the bottleneck once it's \\\"good enough.\\\" The memory hierarchy is.\\n\\nIf that's true, then the highest-value work isn't making `\\u03c0` smarter \\u2014 it's making `\\u03a8` better structured. And the framework we've built here gives you a precise language for specifying what \\\"better structured\\\" means: a rate-distortion-optimal, hierarchically organized, adaptively budgeted epistemic state with selective exogenous observation.\\n\\nA Direct Question\\n-----------------\\n\\nDoes the hierarchical \\u03a8 structure I've sketched here resemble anything you've experimented with in DDIS sharding? I suspect the beads/shard architecture you've been developing is already an implicit solution to this problem in the specification domain, and I'm curious whether you see the isomorphism.\\n\\n---\\n\\n## You asked:\\n\\nYes it does 100%, though the one thing I would say is that there is a degree of plasticity or flexibility that I feel would be optimal that the sharding method lacks. Just as humans don\\u2019t pull in the same formation of epistemic knowledge in every situation, I think an optimized approach would allow this formation to be ad hoc and possibly emergent. I wonder if something like EAV in Datomic is a closer technical analogue to what I\\u2019m trying to imagine. What do you think? Does this make any sense?\\n\\nFeb 28\\n\\n---\\n\\n## Claude Replied:\\n\\nSynthesized EAV model's dynamic plasticity against fixed hierarchical constraints\\n\\nSynthesized EAV model's dynamic plasticity against fixed hierarchical constraints\\n\\nWillem is making a really interesting point. He's saying that the hierarchical structure I proposed is too rigid \\u2014 the layers shouldn't be fixed slots but should be dynamically composed based on the situation. And he's pointing to Datomic's EAV (Entity-Attribute-Value) model as an analogue. Let me think about why that's a powerful reference.\\n\\nDatomic's key properties:\\n\\n1.  Immutable, append-only (facts are never deleted, only retracted by adding new facts)\\n2.  EAV + Time = EAVT \\u2014 every fact is a datom (entity, attribute, value, transaction-time)\\n3.  Queries are declarative (Datalog)\\n4.  The database is a value \\u2014 you can query as-of any point in time\\n5.  Schema is itself data (attributes are entities)\\n\\nThe real insight here is about plasticity: instead of committing upfront to which facts belong in which layer, an EAV model treats everything as raw facts, and the structure emerges from how you query them. Rather than fixed slots with predetermined budgets, you're composing views dynamically based on what's relevant in the moment.\\n\\nThis mirrors how human cognition actually works \\u2014 we don't have rigid compartments for different knowledge types. The same fact can be foundational in one context and peripheral in another, depending on what the situation demands.\\n\\nIn the EAV framework, the core unit is a datom (entity, attribute, value, time), and the context assembly function becomes dynamic rather than a fixed projection \\u2014 it constructs the relevant view on demand from the fact store.\\n\\nThe meta-policy no longer distributes budget across predetermined layers. Instead, it generates task-specific queries that pull together the facts that matter. This is fundamentally a shift from schema-first to schema-on-read thinking about how we organize knowledge.\\n\\nThe free monad connection deepens here: each datom is an atomic unit, and assembly is a fold over the selected facts, where the selection itself is query-driven and task-dependent.\\n\\nShow more\\n\\nDone\\n\\nIt makes complete sense, and I think it's a strictly better model than what I proposed. This is a deep and precise analogy. Let me think about this carefully.\n   909\t\n   910\tSystem 1 = fast, automatic, associative, low-cost \u2192 the lightweight retrieval/query model System 2 = slow, deliberate, logical, expensive \u2192 the LLM reasoner\n   911\t\n   912\tThis maps remarkably well. Let me explore where the analogy holds, where it breaks, and what it predicts about optimal agent architecture.\n   913\t\n   914\tDone\n   915\t\n   916\tThat's a precise analogy and I think it's more than an analogy \u2014 I think it's a structural isomorphism that reveals something important about why current agents are architecturally impoverished.\n   917\t\n   918\tThe Dual-Process Mapping\n   919\t------------------------\n   920\t\n   921\tKahneman's framework, stripped to its formal skeleton:\n   922\t\n   923\t**System 1** \u2014 fast, parallel, associative, low-cost, always running, operates below conscious awareness. It produces _candidates_: impressions, intuitions, primed associations. It doesn't reason \u2014 it _retrieves and pattern-matches_.\n   924\t\n   925\t**System 2** \u2014 slow, sequential, logical, expensive, resource-limited, engaged deliberately. It takes System 1's candidates and subjects them to critical evaluation, planning, and structured reasoning.\n   926\t\n   927\tThe mapping to your agent architecture:\n   928\t\n   929\t    System 1  \u2194  query_for (the retrieval policy)\n   930\t                 - Lightweight, fast\n   931\t                 - Associative: \"given what I'm looking at, \n   932\t                   what else is relevant?\"\n   933\t                 - Runs on every cycle, costs little\n   934\t                 - Produces a VIEW (assembled context \u03a8)\n   935\t    \n   936\t    System 2  \u2194  \u03c0 (the LLM policy/reasoner)\n   937\t                 - Heavyweight, expensive\n   938\t                 - Sequential, deliberative\n   939\t                 - Operates ONLY on what System 1 surfaced\n   940\t                 - Produces ACTIONS (Op + Done)\n   941\t\n   942\tThe critical insight from Kahneman that I think transfers directly: **System 2 doesn't know what System 1 didn't surface.** You can't reason about what isn't in your working memory. System 2's quality is _bounded above_ by System 1's retrieval quality. A brilliant reasoner with a bad retrieval policy is a brilliant reasoner that's always solving the wrong problem.\n   943\t\n   944\tThis is exactly the pathology we identified in current agents: the LLM (System 2) is increasingly capable, but its context (the output of a nearly nonexistent System 1) is assembled by crude mechanisms \u2014 truncation, fixed templates, maybe a single embedding-based RAG pass. **Current agents are almost pure System 2 with a vestigial System 1.**\n   945\t\n   946\tWhat Kahneman Predicts About Agent Failures\n   947\t-------------------------------------------\n   948\t\n   949\tThe dual-process literature catalogs specific failure modes of human cognition. Each one has a precise analogue in the agent architecture, and checking whether agents exhibit these failures is a good validation of the isomorphism:\n   950\t\n   951\t**Availability bias.** Humans overweight information that's easily recalled (recent, vivid, emotional). Agents overweight information that's _close in the context window_ (recent, verbose, dramatic error traces). A long stack trace from a failed build dominates context and crowds out the architectural decision that explains _why_ the build was structured that way. This is System 1 surfacing the vivid over the relevant.\n   952\t\n   953\t**Anchoring.** The first piece of information encountered disproportionately shapes subsequent reasoning. In agents, the system prompt and early conversation turns anchor the entire trajectory. If the initial framing is slightly wrong, System 2 rarely has enough context to revisit it because System 1 keeps surfacing information consistent with the anchor.\n   954\t\n   955\t**Substitution.** When faced with a hard question, System 1 substitutes an easier question and System 2 doesn't notice. Agents do this constantly \u2014 asked to solve a complex architectural problem, they substitute \"make the current error go away\" because the error trace is what's in context (what System 1 surfaced) while the architectural constraint has scrolled away.\n   956\t\n   957\t**Cognitive strain triggers deeper processing.** In humans, when System 1 fails to produce a fluent answer, System 2 engages more carefully. Current agents lack this entirely \u2014 there's no mechanism for the reasoner to signal \"I don't have enough context, I need a different retrieval.\" The agent either works with what it has or fails. There's no upward pressure from System 2 back to System 1 saying \"retrieve differently.\"\n   958\t\n   959\tThe Feedback Loop That's Missing\n   960\t--------------------------------\n   961\t\n   962\tThat last point deserves emphasis because I think it's the most important architectural gap the Kahneman framing reveals. In human cognition, the two systems aren't just sequential \u2014 they're **bidirectional**:\n   963\t\n   964\t    System 1 \u2192 candidates \u2192 System 2 \u2192 actions\n   965\t                                \u2193\n   966\t                            [confusion / low confidence / surprise]\n   967\t                                \u2193\n   968\t                            System 1 (re-retrieval with different cues)\n   969\t\n   970\tWhen System 2 encounters surprise, contradiction, or uncertainty, it can _redirect_ System 1's attention. You're debugging a CSS problem, you hit something unexpected, and suddenly you're remembering a conversation about database schemas from last week \u2014 not because anyone told you to retrieve that, but because System 2's confusion generated new retrieval cues that System 1 resolved associatively.\n   971\t\n   972\tIn the EAV formalization:\n   973\t\n   974\t    -- Normal cycle:\n   975\t    q\u2081 = query_for(task, recent_context)\n   976\t    \u03c8\u2081 = assemble(q\u2081, facts)\n   977\t    action\u2081 = \u03c0(\u03c8\u2081)\n   978\t    \n   979\t    -- Surprise/confusion triggers re-retrieval:\n   980\t    if confidence(\u03c0, \u03c8\u2081) < threshold:\n   981\t        q\u2082 = query_for(task, recent_context \u222a {confusion_signal})\n   982\t        \u03c8\u2082 = assemble(q\u2082, facts)    -- DIFFERENT view, new associations\n   983\t        action\u2082 = \u03c0(\u03c8\u2082)\n   984\t\n   985\tThis is a **closed-loop** between System 1 and System 2. Current agents are **open-loop** \u2014 System 1 fires once, System 2 processes, done. If the retrieval was wrong, there's no recourse except for the full cycle to complete and the next turn to start fresh.\n   986\t\n   987\tWhat an Optimal Architecture Looks Like\n   988\t---------------------------------------\n   989\t\n   990\tPutting this all together, the Kahneman-informed agent architecture would look like:\n   991\t\n   992\t    FactStore : [Datom]              -- append-only EAV (your E*)\n   993\t    S1        : FactStore \u00d7 Cue \u2192 \u03a8  -- System 1 (lightweight, associative)\n   994\t    S2        : \u03a8 \u2192 Action + Confusion  -- System 2 (LLM, deliberative)\n   995\t    \n   996\t    -- Extended action type:\n   997\t    data Action \n   998\t      = Operate Op           -- act on runtime\n   999\t      | Done Result          -- terminate\n  1000\t      \n  1001\t    data Confusion\n  1002\t      = NeedMore Cue         -- re-retrieve with new cue\n  1003\t      | Conflict [Datom]     -- contradictory facts surfaced\n  1004\t      | GoalUnclear          -- lost the thread, need invariants\n  1005\t    \n  1006\t    -- The loop:\n  1007\t    agent_loop(facts, s, task) =\n  1008\t      let cue  = initial_cue(task)\n  1009\t      let \u03c8    = S1(facts, cue)\n  1010\t      match S2(\u03c8) with\n  1011\t      | Operate op \u2192 \n\t< truncated lines 1012-1188 >\n  1189\tThe user is asking a practical question about storage backends for the EAV fact store we've been discussing. Let me think carefully about this rather than making assumptions.\n  1190\t\n  1191\tThe key properties we've established that the store needs:\n  1192\t\n  1193\t1.  Append-only (immutable facts)\n  1194\t2.  Temporal indexing (as-of queries)\n  1195\t3.  Associative retrieval (arbitrary queries over E, A, V)\n  1196\t4.  Schema-on-read (no fixed structure)\n  1197\t5.  Must serve as the universal log E\\*\n  1198\t\n  1199\tLet me compare filesystem, SQL, and EAV-native stores against these requirements.\n  1200\t\n  1201\tShow more\n  1202\t\n  1203\tDone\n  1204\t\n  1205\tThis is a question where I want to resist giving a quick answer because I think the framing reveals something important. Let me work through it from the properties we've established that the store _must_ have, and see which backend satisfies them.\n  1206\t\n  1207\tRequired Properties (From the Formalization)\n  1208\t--------------------------------------------\n  1209\t\n  1210\tThe fact store needs to support:\n  1211\t\n  1212\t**P1 \u2014 Append-only immutability.** Facts are never mutated. Retraction is a new fact. This is the monotonic growth property of `E*`.\n  1213\t\n  1214\t**P2 \u2014 Temporal indexing.** Every fact carries a transaction time. The system must support \"as-of\" queries: \"what did the agent believe at step t?\"\n  1215\t\n  1216\t**P3 \u2014 Associative, multi-index retrieval.** The same fact must be findable by entity, by attribute, by value, and by time. System 1's query cues are unpredictable \u2014 you can't know in advance which index path will be needed.\n  1217\t\n  1218\t**P4 \u2014 Schema-on-read.** No fixed schema. The set of attributes evolves as the agent encounters new kinds of information. Adding a new attribute type must not require migration.\n  1219\t\n  1220\t**P5 \u2014 The store must be accessible from R.** The agent interacts with the store through runtime operations. It needs to be something the agent can read via its existing tool-calling interface.\n  1221\t\n  1222\t**P6 \u2014 Reasonable performance under append-heavy, read-variable workloads.** The write pattern is steady (every agent action appends datoms). The read pattern is bursty and unpredictable (System 1 queries vary per step).\n  1223\t\n  1224\tFilesystem\n  1225\t----------\n  1226\t\n  1227\tA filesystem \u2014 say, the JSONL log that Claude Code currently uses \u2014 satisfies P1 trivially (append to file), P5 trivially (it's a file, readable via standard tools), and arguably P2 (entries are ordered, you can scan to a timestamp).\n  1228\t\n  1229\tBut it fails badly on P3 and P4. A flat file supports exactly one access pattern: sequential scan. Every System 1 query becomes a `grep` or a `jq` filter over the full log. This is O(n) in the number of facts for every retrieval, and it can't do joins. \"Find all facts about entity X where attribute Y changed after time T\" requires a full scan with complex filtering.\n  1230\t\n  1231\tYou _can_ build indexes on top of a filesystem \u2014 but at that point you're implementing a database, poorly. The filesystem is the right _persistence layer_ but the wrong _query layer_.\n  1232\t\n  1233\tRelational SQL\n  1234\t--------------\n  1235\t\n  1236\tSQL satisfies P3 well (indexes, joins, arbitrary WHERE clauses), P6 well (mature query planners, indexing strategies), and P5 reasonably (the agent can query via a tool that issues SQL).\n  1237\t\n  1238\tBut there's a structural mismatch with P4. A relational schema is **schema-on-write**: you define tables and columns upfront. An EAV-style fact \u2014 `(entity: \"file:main.py\", attribute: \"last-error\", value: \"TypeError on line 42\", time: t\u2083\u2087)` \u2014 doesn't naturally fit a fixed table structure.\n  1239\t\n  1240\tYou _can_ implement EAV in SQL. It's a well-known pattern:\n  1241\t\n  1242\tsql\n  1243\t\n  1244\t    CREATE TABLE facts (\n  1245\t      entity    TEXT,\n  1246\t      attribute TEXT,\n  1247\t      value     TEXT,    -- everything serialized to text\n  1248\t      tx_time   INTEGER,\n  1249\t      asserted  BOOLEAN\n  1250\t    );\n  1251\t    \n  1252\t    CREATE INDEX idx_eav ON facts(entity, attribute);\n  1253\t    CREATE INDEX idx_aev ON facts(attribute, entity);\n  1254\t    CREATE INDEX idx_time ON facts(tx_time);\n  1255\t\n  1256\tThis works. But you immediately hit the classic EAV-in-SQL problems. Value typing is gone \u2014 everything is text, so numeric comparisons, range queries, and type-safe joins require casting or auxiliary tables. Queries become verbose and repetitive. The query planner can't optimize as well because the schema carries no semantic information about what attributes mean or how they relate.\n  1257\t\n  1258\tMore fundamentally, SQL's query language assumes **known schema**. Writing correct SQL against an EAV table requires knowing which attribute strings exist, what their value types are, and how entities relate. This pushes schema knowledge into the _query author_ \u2014 which in our architecture is either System 1 (a lightweight model) or the LLM itself. That's a lot of incidental complexity to impose on the retrieval policy.\n  1259\t\n  1260\tWhat the Formalization Actually Asks For\n  1261\t----------------------------------------\n  1262\t\n  1263\tHere's where I want to be careful about not making assumptions. Let me state what I think the formalization demands and let you push back.\n  1264\t\n  1265\tThe ideal store is one where:\n  1266\t\n  1267\t1.  **The write path is trivial.** Appending a datom should be as simple as appending to a log. No schema validation, no constraint checking, no migration. The agent produces facts at high frequency and the store should impose zero friction.\n  1268\t2.  **The read path is expressive and multi-modal.** System 1 needs to query by entity (\"everything about file X\"), by attribute (\"all errors\"), by time range (\"what happened in the last 50 steps\"), by value pattern (\"any fact mentioning function Y\"), and by arbitrary conjunctions of these.\n  1269\t3.  **The query language is LLM-friendly.** If we're using Approach 2 or 3 from our earlier discussion \u2014 where the LLM generates or parameterizes queries \u2014 the query language needs to be something the LLM can produce reliably. This is a practical constraint but a real one.\n  1270\t4.  **Temporal semantics are native, not bolted on.** \"What did the agent believe at step t?\" should be a primitive operation, not a hand-rolled query with timestamp filters.\n  1271\t\n  1272\tTaking these together, I think the honest answer is: **neither a raw filesystem nor a conventional SQL database is the right fit, but SQL gets you closer, and a Datalog engine over an EAV store is what the architecture actually wants.**\n  1273\t\n  1274\tThe Practical Spectrum\n  1275\t----------------------\n  1276\t\n  1277\tLet me give you what I see as the options ordered from most pragmatic to most principled:\n  1278\t\n  1279\t**Option A \u2014 SQLite with EAV schema.** Available everywhere, zero deployment overhead, the agent can query it via tool calls that execute SQL. You lose value typing and temporal semantics are manual, but it's buildable today and satisfies P1-P6 adequately. This is the \"good enough\" option and honestly might be the right starting point for validation.\n  1280\t\n  1281\t**Option B \u2014 DuckDB with EAV schema.** Same deployment story as SQLite but better analytical query performance, better JSON/nested type support. If the value column carries structured data (and it will \u2014 observations from tool calls are complex), DuckDB handles this more gracefully than SQLite.\n  1282\t\n  1283\t**Option C \u2014 An embedded Datalog engine.** Something like Datascript (the in-memory Clojure implementation of Datomic's data model) or its equivalents. This gives you native EAV, native temporal indexing, and a query language (Datalog) that is both more expressive than SQL for graph-like associative queries and arguably easier for an LLM to generate correctly \u2014 Datalog queries are declarative pattern matches, not imperative join specifications.\n  1284\t\n  1285\t**Option D \u2014 Actual Datomic (or XTDB).** The full realization of the architecture. Immutable, temporally indexed, schema-as-data, Datalog queries, built from the ground up for exactly this data model. XTDB in particular is open source and embeddable. The cost is JVM dependency and operational complexity.\n  1286\t\n  1287\tMy Assessment\n  1288\t-------------\n  1289\t\n  1290\tIf I'm being principled: the formalization asks for Option C or D. The data model is EAV, the access patterns are associative and temporal, and the query language should be declarative pattern matching. Bolting this onto SQL is possible but introduces impedance mismatch at every layer.\n  1291\t\n  1292\tIf I'm being practical: Option A (SQLite + EAV) is where I'd start, because the first thing you need to validate is whether the _architecture_ (dual-process loop, confusion detection, dynamic context assembly) improves agent performance. The storage backend is a replaceable component. If the architecture validates, you migrate to a native EAV store. If it doesn't, you haven't invested in infrastructure you don't need.\n  1293\t\n  1294\tBut I want to flag an uncertainty: I'm reasoning about this from the algebraic properties and from general database knowledge. I don't have direct experience benchmarking Datalog query generation by LLMs versus SQL query generation. If LLMs are significantly worse at producing correct Datalog than correct SQL, that could flip the practical calculus. This is a deep and precise analogy. Let me think about this carefully.\\n\\nSystem 1 = fast, automatic, associative, low-cost \\u2192 the lightweight retrieval/query model System 2 = slow, deliberate, logical, expensive \\u2192 the LLM reasoner\\n\\nThis maps remarkably well. Let me explore where the analogy holds, where it breaks, and what it predicts about optimal agent architecture.\\n\\nDone\\n\\nThat's a precise analogy and I think it's more than an analogy \\u2014 I think it's a structural isomorphism that reveals something important about why current agents are architecturally impoverished.\\n\\nThe Dual-Process Mapping\\n------------------------\\n\\nKahneman's framework, stripped to its formal skeleton:\\n\\n**System 1** \\u2014 fast, parallel, associative, low-cost, always running, operates below conscious awareness. It produces _candidates_: impressions, intuitions, primed associations. It doesn't reason \\u2014 it _retrieves and pattern-matches_.\\n\\n**System 2** \\u2014 slow, sequential, logical, expensive, resource-limited, engaged deliberately. It takes System 1's candidates and subjects them to critical evaluation, planning, and structured reasoning.\\n\\nThe mapping to your agent architecture:\\n\\n    System 1  \\u2194  query_for (the retrieval policy)\\n                 - Lightweight, fast\\n                 - Associative: \\\"given what I'm looking at, \\n                   what else is relevant?\\\"\\n                 - Runs on every cycle, costs little\\n                 - Produces a VIEW (assembled context \\u03a8)\\n    \\n    System 2  \\u2194  \\u03c0 (the LLM policy/reasoner)\\n                 - Heavyweight, expensive\\n                 - Sequential, deliberative\\n                 - Operates ONLY on what System 1 surfaced\\n                 - Produces ACTIONS (Op + Done)\\n\\nThe critical insight from Kahneman that I think transfers directly: **System 2 doesn't know what System 1 didn't surface.** You can't reason about what isn't in your working memory. System 2's quality is _bounded above_ by System 1's retrieval quality. A brilliant reasoner with a bad retrieval policy is a brilliant reasoner that's always solving the wrong problem.\\n\\nThis is exactly the pathology we identified in current agents: the LLM (System 2) is increasingly capable, but its context (the output of a nearly nonexistent System 1) is assembled by crude mechanisms \\u2014 truncation, fixed templates, maybe a single embedding-based RAG pass. **Current agents are almost pure System 2 with a vestigial System 1.**\\n\\nWhat Kahneman Predicts About Agent Failures\\n-------------------------------------------\\n\\nThe dual-process literature catalogs specific failure modes of human cognition. Each one has a precise analogue in the agent architecture, and checking whether agents exhibit these failures is a good validation of the isomorphism:\\n\\n**Availability bias.** Humans overweight information that's easily recalled (recent, vivid, emotional). Agents overweight information that's _close in the context window_ (recent, verbose, dramatic error traces). A long stack trace from a failed build dominates context and crowds out the architectural decision that explains _why_ the build was structured that way. This is System 1 surfacing the vivid over the relevant.\\n\\n**Anchoring.** The first piece of information encountered disproportionately shapes subsequent reasoning. In agents, the system prompt and early conversation turns anchor the entire trajectory. If the initial framing is slightly wrong, System 2 rarely has enough context to revisit it because System 1 keeps surfacing information consistent with the anchor.\\n\\n**Substitution.** When faced with a hard question, System 1 substitutes an easier question and System 2 doesn't notice. Agents do this constantly \\u2014 asked to solve a complex architectural problem, they substitute \\\"make the current error go away\\\" because the error trace is what's in context (what System 1 surfaced) while the architectural constraint has scrolled away.\\n\\n**Cognitive strain triggers deeper processing.** In humans, when System 1 fails to produce a fluent answer, System 2 engages more carefully. Current agents lack this entirely \\u2014 there's no mechanism for the reasoner to signal \\\"I don't have enough context, I need a different retrieval.\\\" The agent either works with what it has or fails. There's no upward pressure from System 2 back to System 1 saying \\\"retrieve differently.\\\"\\n\\nThe Feedback Loop That's Missing\\n--------------------------------\\n\\nThat last point deserves emphasis because I think it's the most important architectural gap the Kahneman framing reveals. In human cognition, the two systems aren't just sequential \\u2014 they're **bidirectional**:\\n\\n    System 1 \\u2192 candidates \\u2192 System 2 \\u2192 actions\\n                                \\u2193\\n                            [confusion / low confidence / surprise]\\n                                \\u2193\\n                            System 1 (re-retrieval with different cues)\\n\\nWhen System 2 encounters surprise, contradiction, or uncertainty, it can _redirect_ System 1's attention. You're debugging a CSS problem, you hit something unexpected, and suddenly you're remembering a conversation about database schemas from last week \\u2014 not because anyone told you to retrieve that, but because System 2's confusion generated new retrieval cues that System 1 resolved associatively.\\n\\nIn the EAV formalization:\\n\\n    -- Normal cycle:\\n    q\\u2081 = query_for(task, recent_context)\\n    \\u03c8\\u2081 = assemble(q\\u2081, facts)\\n    action\\u2081 = \\u03c0(\\u03c8\\u2081)\\n    \\n    -- Surprise/confusion triggers re-retrieval:\\n    if confidence(\\u03c0, \\u03c8\\u2081) < threshold:\\n        q\\u2082 = query_for(task, recent_context \\u222a {confusion_signal})\\n        \\u03c8\\u2082 = assemble(q\\u2082, facts)    -- DIFFERENT view, new associations\\n        action\\u2082 = \\u03c0(\\u03c8\\u2082)\\n\\nThis is a **closed-loop** between System 1 and System 2. Current agents are **open-loop** \\u2014 System 1 fires once, System 2 processes, done. If the retrieval was wrong, there's no recourse except for the full cycle to complete and the next turn to start fresh.\\n\\nWhat an Optimal Architecture Looks Like\\n---------------------------------------\\n\\nPutting this all together, the Kahneman-informed agent architecture would look like:\\n\\n    FactStore : [Datom]              -- append-only EAV (your E*)\\n    S1        : FactStore \\u00d7 Cue \\u2192 \\u03a8  -- System 1 (lightweight, associative)\\n    S2        : \\u03a8 \\u2192 Action + Confusion  -- System 2 (LLM, deliberative)\\n    \\n    -- Extended action type:\\n    data Action \\n      = Operate Op           -- act on runtime\\n      | Done Result          -- terminate\\n      \\n    data Confusion\\n      = NeedMore Cue         -- re-retrieve with new cue\\n      | Conflict [Datom]     -- contradictory facts surfaced\\n      | GoalUnclear          -- lost the thread, need invariants\\n    \\n    -- The loop:\\n    agent_loop(facts, s, task) =\\n      let cue  = initial_cue(task)\\n      let \\u03c8    = S1(facts, cue)\\n      match S2(\\u03c8) with\\n      | Operate op \\u2192 \\n< truncated lines 1012-1188 >\\nThe user is asking a practical question about storage backends for the EAV fact store we've been discussing. Let me think carefully about this rather than making assumptions.\\n\\nThe key properties we've established that the store needs:\\n\\n1.  Append-only (immutable facts)\\n2.  Temporal indexing (as-of queries)\\n3.  Associative retrieval (arbitrary queries over E, A, V)\\n4.  Schema-on-read (no fixed structure)\\n5.  Must serve as the universal log E\\\\*\\n\\nLet me compare filesystem, SQL, and EAV-native stores against these requirements.\\n\\nShow more\\n\\nDone\\n\\nThis is a question where I want to resist giving a quick answer because I think the framing reveals something important. Let me work through it from the properties we've established that the store _must_ have, and see which backend satisfies them.\\n\\nRequired Properties (From the Formalization)\\n--------------------------------------------\\n\\nThe fact store needs to support:\\n\\n**P1 \\u2014 Append-only immutability.** Facts are never mutated. Retraction is a new fact. This is the monotonic growth property of `E*`.\\n\\n**P2 \\u2014 Temporal indexing.** Every fact carries a transaction time. The system must support \\\"as-of\\\" queries: \\\"what did the agent believe at step t?\\\"\\n\\n**P3 \\u2014 Associative, multi-index retrieval.** The same fact must be findable by entity, by attribute, by value, and by time. System 1's query cues are unpredictable \\u2014 you can't know in advance which index path will be needed.\\n\\n**P4 \\u2014 Schema-on-read.** No fixed schema. The set of attributes evolves as the agent encounters new kinds of information. Adding a new attribute type must not require migration.\\n\\n**P5 \\u2014 The store must be accessible from R.** The agent interacts with the store through runtime operations. It needs to be something the agent can read via its existing tool-calling interface.\\n\\n**P6 \\u2014 Reasonable performance under append-heavy, read-variable workloads.** The write pattern is steady (every agent action appends datoms). The read pattern is bursty and unpredictable (System 1 queries vary per step).\\n\\nFilesystem\\n----------\\n\\nA filesystem \\u2014 say, the JSONL log that Claude Code currently uses \\u2014 satisfies P1 trivially (append to file), P5 trivially (it's a file, readable via standard tools), and arguably P2 (entries are ordered, you can scan to a timestamp).\\n\\nBut it fails badly on P3 and P4. A flat file supports exactly one access pattern: sequential scan. Every System 1 query becomes a `grep` or a `jq` filter over the full log. This is O(n) in the number of facts for every retrieval, and it can't do joins. \\\"Find all facts about entity X where attribute Y changed after time T\\\" requires a full scan with complex filtering.\\n\\nYou _can_ build indexes on top of a filesystem \\u2014 but at that point you're implementing a database, poorly. The filesystem is the right _persistence layer_ but the wrong _query layer_.\\n\\nRelational SQL\\n--------------\\n\\nSQL satisfies P3 well (indexes, joins, arbitrary WHERE clauses), P6 well (mature query planners, indexing strategies), and P5 reasonably (the agent can query via a tool that issues SQL).\\n\\nBut there's a structural mismatch with P4. A relational schema is **schema-on-write**: you define tables and columns upfront. An EAV-style fact \\u2014 `(entity: \\\"file:main.py\\\", attribute: \\\"last-error\\\", value: \\\"TypeError on line 42\\\", time: t\\u2083\\u2087)` \\u2014 doesn't naturally fit a fixed table structure.\\n\\nYou _can_ implement EAV in SQL. It's a well-known pattern:\\n\\nsql\\n\\n    CREATE TABLE facts (\\n      entity    TEXT,\\n      attribute TEXT,\\n      value     TEXT,    -- everything serialized to text\\n      tx_time   INTEGER,\\n      asserted  BOOLEAN\\n    );\\n    \\n    CREATE INDEX idx_eav ON facts(entity, attribute);\\n    CREATE INDEX idx_aev ON facts(attribute, entity);\\n    CREATE INDEX idx_time ON facts(tx_time);\\n\\nThis works. But you immediately hit the classic EAV-in-SQL problems. Value typing is gone \\u2014 everything is text, so numeric comparisons, range queries, and type-safe joins require casting or auxiliary tables. Queries become verbose and repetitive. The query planner can't optimize as well because the schema carries no semantic information about what attributes mean or how they relate.\\n\\nMore fundamentally, SQL's query language assumes **known schema**. Writing correct SQL against an EAV table requires knowing which attribute strings exist, what their value types are, and how entities relate. This pushes schema knowledge into the _query author_ \\u2014 which in our architecture is either System 1 (a lightweight model) or the LLM itself. That's a lot of incidental complexity to impose on the retrieval policy.\\n\\nWhat the Formalization Actually Asks For\\n----------------------------------------\\n\\nHere's where I want to be careful about not making assumptions. Let me state what I think the formalization demands and let you push back.\\n\\nThe ideal store is one where:\\n\\n1.  **The write path is trivial.** Appending a datom should be as simple as appending to a log. No schema validation, no constraint checking, no migration. The agent produces facts at high frequency and the store should impose zero friction.\\n2.  **The read path is expressive and multi-modal.** System 1 needs to query by entity (\\\"everything about file X\\\"), by attribute (\\\"all errors\\\"), by time range (\\\"what happened in the last 50 steps\\\"), by value pattern (\\\"any fact mentioning function Y\\\"), and by arbitrary conjunctions of these.\\n3.  **The query language is LLM-friendly.** If we're using Approach 2 or 3 from our earlier discussion \\u2014 where the LLM generates or parameterizes queries \\u2014 the query language needs to be something the LLM can produce reliably. This is a practical constraint but a real one.\\n4.  **Temporal semantics are native, not bolted on.** \\\"What did the agent believe at step t?\\\" should be a primitive operation, not a hand-rolled query with timestamp filters.\\n\\nTaking these together, I think the honest answer is: **neither a raw filesystem nor a conventional SQL database is the right fit, but SQL gets you closer, and a Datalog engine over an EAV store is what the architecture actually wants.**\\n\\nThe Practical Spectrum\\n----------------------\\n\\nLet me give you what I see as the options ordered from most pragmatic to most principled:\\n\\n**Option A \\u2014 SQLite with EAV schema.** Available everywhere, zero deployment overhead, the agent can query it via tool calls that execute SQL. You lose value typing and temporal semantics are manual, but it's buildable today and satisfies P1-P6 adequately. This is the \\\"good enough\\\" option and honestly might be the right starting point for validation.\\n\\n**Option B \\u2014 DuckDB with EAV schema.** Same deployment story as SQLite but better analytical query performance, better JSON/nested type support. If the value column carries structured data (and it will \\u2014 observations from tool calls are complex), DuckDB handles this more gracefully than SQLite.\\n\\n**Option C \\u2014 An embedded Datalog engine.** Something like Datascript (the in-memory Clojure implementation of Datomic's data model) or its equivalents. This gives you native EAV, native temporal indexing, and a query language (Datalog) that is both more expressive than SQL for graph-like associative queries and arguably easier for an LLM to generate correctly \\u2014 Datalog queries are declarative pattern matches, not imperative join specifications.\\n\\n**Option D \\u2014 Actual Datomic (or XTDB).** The full realization of the architecture. Immutable, temporally indexed, schema-as-data, Datalog queries, built from the ground up for exactly this data model. XTDB in particular is open source and embeddable. The cost is JVM dependency and operational complexity.\\n\\nMy Assessment\\n-------------\\n\\nIf I'm being principled: the formalization asks for Option C or D. The data model is EAV, the access patterns are associative and temporal, and the query language should be declarative pattern matching. Bolting this onto SQL is possible but introduces impedance mismatch at every layer.\\n\\nIf I'm being practical: Option A (SQLite + EAV) is where I'd start, because the first thing you need to validate is whether the _architecture_ (dual-process loop, confusion detection, dynamic context assembly) improves agent performance. The storage backend is a replaceable component. If the architecture validates, you migrate to a native EAV store. If it doesn't, you haven't invested in infrastructure you don't need.\\n\\nBut I want to flag an uncertainty: I'm reasoning about this from the algebraic properties and from general database knowledge. I don't have direct experience benchmarking Datalog query generation by LLMs versus SQL query generation. If LLMs are significantly worse at producing correct Datalog than correct SQL, that could flip the practical calculus.
